## Prompt

Message ID: `d6997e0d-f44c-4604-afe6-0cd2331f3f3e`
Created UTC: `2026-03-30T22:48:38.241000Z`

for this part, where i want to show my friend the play from the screenshots above he sent me, as a tracker, triggering thos dialogue clusters through speech synths

## Response

Message ID: `9d687a79-df8c-4140-92de-c0407fcb5b2e`
Created UTC: `2026-03-30T22:49:06.346113Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

Yes. **For that specific demonstration, use Max first.**

Not because Max is metaphysically superior, but because this is a **showable instrument problem**, not yet a product-surface problem. You want to take the play from those screenshots and present it as a tracker-like speech score where dialogue clusters fire through synthesized voices. Max is the fastest route to something your friend can see, hear, and understand in one sitting.

#### What the demo should be

Do **not** build “the app” for this step.

Build a **single-purpose score-player patch**:

A four-lane tracker surface, one lane per voice-channel. The play material is broken into `$PHRASE_EVENT`s and small cluster units. A transport runs top-to-bottom by rows. When the playhead hits an occupied cell, that lane speaks the assigned phrase. That is already fully aligned with the system direction: phrase-events, voice-channels, temporal relation, timing substrate, and score-encoding of speech. [filecite: turn5file17turn5file19]

#### Why Max is the right choice here

For this exact use case, you need three things fast.

First, a visible grid.

Second, deterministic triggering.

Third, immediate audio feedback with controllable routing.

Max gives you those without forcing you to solve the whole browser/version/share problem yet. Your own project material already distinguishes the deeper composition engine from the later product surface, and this is clearly a **composition-and-execution proof**, not yet the full listening-first workbench. [filecite: turn5file1turn5file9]

#### The right prototype shape

Treat the piece as a **tracker score of clustered utterance-events**.

Use these lanes:

`$VOICE_CHANNEL_01 = FIRST_WOMAN`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_02 = SECOND_WOMAN`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_03 = GLASS`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_04 = BAKER`

Then break the text into cluster families rather than preserving only ordinary line order. The screenshots already imply that the work mutates from readable dialogue into distributed repetition fields, so your tracker version should honor that by storing not just full lines, but also decomposed fragments and recombinable clusters. That is exactly the move from literary line to `$PHRASE_EVENT`. [filecite: turn5file7turn5file19]

So your content bank should probably have three levels.

Level one is full-line events for the initial readable exchange.

Level two is phrase clusters for repeated modules.

Level three is atomized fragments for the later dispersal field.

#### What the first demo should look like

The strongest first pass is not a dense Renoise clone. It is a **clean, slow, legible vertical sequencer**.

Across the top: the four voice-channels.

Down the left: row numbers.

In each cell: either empty, a short cluster code, or a compact text label.

At the bottom: tempo, row rate, start/stop, section jump, and a mode selector.

Then give yourself three modes.

Mode one is `$READABLE_PASSAGE`. This plays the scene in conventional order, but as a tracker.

Mode two is `$CLUSTER_PASSAGE`. This begins repeating and redistributing the dialogue clusters.

Mode three is `$DISPERSAL_PASSAGE`. This atomizes the text and spreads fragments across channels and rows.

That alone will make the formal idea obvious to your friend.

#### How to build it in Max

The minimal patch architecture should be:

`[transport]` or a master timing clock
`[metro]` driving row advance
`[counter]` for current row
`[coll]` or `[dict]` storing score rows
`[route]` by voice-channel
speech renderer per channel
mixer and optional pan per channel
a grid UI such as `[matrixctrl]` or a custom Jitter display

The important design decision is that each row should resolve to a small event object, not raw text only. Even in Max, keep the ontology clean.

A row event should look more like this:

```json
{
  "row": 24,
  "voice": "GLASS",
  "text": "need loaf bread",
  "mode": "cluster",
  "pause_after_ms": 180,
  "repeat": 2,
  "pan": 0.2,
  "rate": 1.0
}
```

That keeps the demo structurally faithful to the larger system instead of turning it into a loose trigger toy. Your docs already insist that scenes need to be stored as structured temporal dialogue objects rather than plain documents; this is that principle in miniature. [filecite: turn5file6]

#### Speech synthesis choice

For the demo, you have two viable routes.

Route one is **pre-rendered speech samples**. This is the safest. Render each phrase or cluster once, load them into Max, and trigger them like samples. That gives you stable timing and avoids TTS latency.

Route two is **live speech synthesis**. This is more impressive, but riskier in a demo. If you do this, keep the vocabulary small and cache outputs aggressively.

For showing your friend, I would choose **pre-rendered phrase banks first**. Then the tracker behaves musically and reliably. You are proving the score logic, not the cloud dependency.

#### How to map the screenshots into tracker structure

Do not try to preserve every page visually on day one.

Instead, translate the material into three section blocks.

Section A is the recognizable exchange. One row or one small row-group per utterance. Clear channel identity. Moderate tempo.

Section B is recurrent cluster buildup. Same materials return in alternating or stacked positions. Repeats and offsets become visible.

Section C is distributed field behavior. Phrases fragment into shorter units and appear across channels in a more score-like pattern.

That mapping is already implied by your source concept: text becoming duration, cue, repetition pattern, and voice assignment instead of remaining inert script. [filecite: turn5file15]

#### The specific tracker commands worth using

For this first patch, only implement a few commands.

`PA` for pause after event.
`RP` for repeat count.
`OV` for overlap enable.
`PN` for pan.
`EM` for emphasis or gain boost.
`CU` for cueing another lane one or more rows later.

Those are enough to demonstrate that the piece is not just “read aloud,” but **composed**. Your tracker notes already define this exact shift: row-level commands are where dialogue playback becomes compositional infrastructure. [filecite: turn5file11]

#### What I would not do yet

Do not build version history.

Do not build diagnostics.

Do not build a share link system.

Do not build parse automation from the screenshots.

Just hard-code the play into a tracker-score dataset and make it sing.

That restraint is important. The project materials are clear that the first proof should not get buried under later-layer complexity. [filecite: turn5file2turn5file10]

#### Best concrete plan

Build `$PROTO_PLAY_01` as a Max patch with four speech lanes, about 64 to 128 rows, and three scene states: readable, clustered, dispersed.

Use a single master clock.

Store the score in `dict` or `coll`.

Use pre-rendered phrase audio for reliability.

Display the active row and highlighted cells clearly.

Let your friend mute and solo lanes, change tempo, and jump between the three sections.

That is enough to prove the thesis.

#### Bottom line

For **this part**, yes: use **Max/MSP**.

Make a **trackerized speech-score patch**, not a full app. Treat the dialogue as clustered `$PHRASE_EVENT`s across four `$VOICE_CHANNEL`s, trigger them row-by-row, and let the form evolve from readable exchange into distributed repetition. That will communicate the idea immediately and cleanly. [filecite: turn5file17turn5file19]

I can draft the exact **row schema and section map** for the play next, so you can start patching it directly.


## dramaturgist-tuning - 07 Prototype & Proof of Concept -- 004 Prompt / Response

File: [dramaturgist-tuning-07--prototype-and-proof-of-concept--004-prompt-response.md](dramaturgist-tuning-07--prototype-and-proof-of-concept--004-prompt-response.md)  
Pair ID: `dt-07-004`  
Content SHA-256: `d079ec4c1cf9671cb674a1832fb8eb2e6247a1daa817fbd67114468cb420221c`

## Prompt

Message ID: `b15365da-03db-4b14-b9a8-8ce59eb8366a`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:44:45.079000Z`

voice synthesis is injectable?

## Response

Message ID: `dbeaf755-a258-4ae0-8b9a-7cbef1a3b448`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:44:52.078691Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

#### Yes

Voice synthesis should be treated as **injectable infrastructure**, not baked into the score model.

The tracker patch should emit a normalized speech event, and then a separate voice layer should decide how that event becomes sound. That keeps the piece portable and lets you swap between pre-rendered samples, OS speech, local synthesis, or remote TTS without changing the score itself.

In your case, the clean separation is:

`$PHRASE_EVENT -> $VOICE_RENDER_ADAPTER -> audio output`

So the tracker only knows things like `$VOICE_CHANNEL`, `$TEXT`, `$ROW`, `$PAUSE_AFTER_MS`, `$RATE`, `$PAN`, `$REPEAT`. It should **not** know whether the sound came from Apple speech, ElevenLabs, a local model, prerecorded WAVs, or anything else.

#### In Max terms

Yes, you can inject voice synthesis into the patch in at least three practical ways.

First, the safest route is `$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE=sample_cache`. The patch triggers pre-rendered phrase files from `$VOICE_CACHE_DIR`. This is best for the demo because timing is stable and there is no network delay.

Second, you can use `$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE=live_tts`. The patch sends text to a synthesis adapter through Node for Max, `js`, OSC, or HTTP, and receives audio back. This is more flexible but less stable in a live demo.

Third, you can use `$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE=hybrid`. If a phrase exists in cache, play it immediately. If not, request synthesis, write the result into `$VOICE_CACHE_DIR`, and use the cached version on the next pass. That is usually the smartest architecture.

#### What “injectable” should mean structurally

It should mean that the same score can run against multiple backends by changing environment variables, not by rewriting the patch.

For example:

`$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE=sample_cache`
`$TTS_PROVIDER_URI=http://127.0.0.1:8787/render`
`$VOICE_CACHE_DIR=/Users/you/audio-cache/speech-score`
`$DEFAULT_VOICE_PROFILE_ID=glass-neutral-01`
`$FALLBACK_VOICE_PROFILE_ID=narrator-flat-01`

Then each lane or `$VOICE_CHANNEL` can also carry its own voice assignment:

`$VOICE_CHANNEL_01_PROFILE=first-woman-bright-01`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_02_PROFILE=second-woman-dry-01`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_03_PROFILE=glass-measured-01`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_04_PROFILE=baker-warm-01`

That is the right meaning of injectable here: the patch stays the same; the rendering backend changes.

#### For your friend-demo

Use injection, but do it conservatively.

For the first proof, I would not rely on real-time cloud speech. I would inject the voice layer as a **cached renderer**. The tracker fires cluster events; the adapter resolves them to pre-rendered files. That will feel instantaneous and musical.

Then, once the demo works, you can swap `$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE` to live synthesis and test dynamic text generation later.

#### The best internal contract

Make every tracker cell resolve to something like this:

```json
{
  "phrase_id": "glass_024",
  "voice_channel": "GLASS",
  "text": "Yes. I need a loaf of bread.",
  "voice_profile_id": "glass-measured-01",
  "start_row": 24,
  "pause_after_ms": 160,
  "repeat": 1,
  "pan": 0.15,
  "render_mode": "cache_preferred"
}
```

Then the adapter does the rest.

That is the key move. The score remains a score. Voice synthesis becomes a pluggable render service.

#### Bottom line

Yes. It should be injectable.

For your Max prototype, that means: **do not hardwire the synthesis engine into the tracker logic**. Hardwire only the event schema and the timing. Inject the voice backend through `$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE`, `$TTS_PROVIDER_URI`, `$VOICE_CACHE_DIR`, and per-channel `$VOICE_PROFILE_ID`s.

For the demo you described, cached phrase rendering is the most reliable version of that idea.

## Prompt

Message ID: `7f4e01cb-3559-47fb-b298-4b9a2271bfe1`

how much audio of voice speaking is required to create a synthetic copy of recording?

## Response

##### Response Segment 1

Message ID: `aafbcfec-6149-46b2-b104-951a72fc4830`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:46:03.692763Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

I’m checking current provider guidance rather than guessing, because the minimum usable sample length varies a lot by cloning method and quality target.

##### Response Segment 2

Message ID: `54dd9095-f60a-4f0d-b43a-b2f516e17cc5`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:46:23.520571Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

I have enough to answer now. The current providers are all over the map: “instant similarity” clones can work from seconds, while stable long-form or premium clones usually want tens of minutes to hours.

##### Response Segment 3

Message ID: `501fc6e9-f9b7-4126-989c-16db30421e82`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:46:23.520571Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

#### It depends on what you mean by “synthetic copy”

If you mean a **quick, recognizable voice clone**, current systems can often get something usable from **just a few seconds to a couple of minutes** of clean speech. Cartesia’s cloning docs say a high-similarity clone can use a clip of about **5 seconds**, and Resemble’s “rapid clone” is documented at **10 seconds to 3 minutes**. ElevenLabs’ instant voice cloning guidance is stricter and recommends about **1–2 minutes** of clear audio. [cite: turn879704search4turn879704search0turn194595search6turn194595search1

If you mean a **high-quality, stable, production-grade clone** that holds up across longer passages and captures more nuance, the requirement rises a lot. Resemble documents **10–25+ minutes** for professional cloning; Cartesia says its pro clone requires at least **30 minutes** and recommends about **2 hours**; ElevenLabs recommends **30–180 minutes**, with guidance pointing toward **1–3 hours** for best results. citeturn879704search0turn879704search6turn194595search0turn194595search1

#### Practical rule of thumb

For planning purposes, treat it like this: **5–30 seconds** can be enough for a rough similarity demo; **1–3 minutes** is a common target for a decent instant clone; **10–30+ minutes** is where more serious trained clones start; **30–180 minutes** is the range to expect for a near-professional or long-form voice model. citeturn879704search4turn879704search0turn194595search6turn194595search0turn879704search6]

#### What matters as much as duration

Providers repeatedly emphasize that **audio quality and consistency matter at least as much as raw runtime**. They want **one speaker only**, minimal background noise, little reverb, trimmed silence, and speech in the target style and language; otherwise the clone tends to reproduce the recording defects too. ElevenLabs says the total runtime matters more than the number of clips and recommends clear single-speaker audio; Cartesia similarly says longer clips do not necessarily improve the clone and recommends short, clean speech with limited pauses. [cite: turn194595search0turn194595search7turn879704search1

#### For your use case

For a **tracker-style play demo with dialogue clusters**, you do **not** need a perfect long-form clone. A short, recognizable clone or even a stylized synthetic proxy is enough, so **10 seconds to 2 minutes per voice** is usually the realistic minimum zone to test the idea. If you want it to sound convincingly like a specific person across a whole performance, budget **30 minutes or more**, preferably much more. citeturn879704search0turn194595search6turn194595search0]

#### One constraint worth stating plainly

If the voice is **not your own**, many platforms require permission, and some professional-clone workflows restrict creation to the speaker verifying their own voice. ElevenLabs, for example, says instant cloning requires that you have the right and consent to clone the voice, and its professional cloning flow is limited to **your own verified voice**. [cite: turn194595search6turn194595search4turn194595search3]

If you want, I can map this into a concrete capture plan like `4 voices × target sample length × recording settings × cache strategy` for your Max patch.

##### Response Segment 1

Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:48:28.367542Z`

##### Response Segment 2

Message ID: `f32ba670-bf5f-40f5-b549-2e9ac60ef8a5`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:49:01.780975Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

Yes. For **internal rehearsal or training while traveling**, that is a sensible use case — but structure it as a **company voice library with explicit performer permission**, not as ad hoc scraping of old recordings.

#### The practical constraint

The main operational issue is that **provider rules differ**. ElevenLabs’ instant cloning flow requires you to confirm you have the **right and consent** to clone the voice, while ElevenLabs’ **professional** cloning is limited to **your own verified voice** and recommends much longer training audio. That means a theatre company can use instant-style internal clones more easily than “professional” clones on that platform, but not every provider treats third-party company voices the same way. [cite: turn632349search0turn632349search5turn632349search4]

#### The capture target I would use

For your use case, I would split the voice bank into two quality tiers.

For a **travel/training tier**, capture about **1–3 minutes of clean speech per performer**. That is enough to land in the workable range for current “instant” workflows: ElevenLabs recommends roughly **1–2 minutes** of clear audio for instant cloning, Resemble documents **10 seconds** for rapid clone, and Cartesia’s clone endpoint says a high-similarity clone can use about **5 seconds**, with its broader best-practices page recommending clips around **10 seconds**. [cite: turn632349search0turn496678search3turn632349search6turn632349search8]

For a **durable house-library tier**, I would capture **10–30 minutes per performer at minimum**, and preferably more if you want stronger long-form stability. That recommendation is an inference from current provider guidance: Resemble’s professional clone flow calls for **20+ recordings totaling at least 10 minutes**, while ElevenLabs’ professional guidance recommends **30 minutes bare minimum**, with **1–3 hours** preferred for best results. [cite: turn496678search3turn496678search0turn632349search4]

#### The recording spec

What matters almost as much as runtime is **cleanliness and consistency**. The docs are very consistent on this point: use **one speaker only**, keep the room quiet, avoid reverb, avoid long silences, and keep the delivery style reasonably consistent because the model will imitate the speaking style, pacing, and artifacts it hears. ElevenLabs and Cartesia both explicitly say noise, pauses, and performance style will be reflected in the clone. [cite: turn632349search0turn496678search0turn632349search8]

So for each actor, I would record one neutral rehearsal script, one expressive script closer to their stage energy, and one short “control” script with the exact diction you care about for the piece. Then I would keep only the cleanest takes and trim them tightly.

#### The best company workflow

For your theatre-company case, the cleanest model is this:

Record the performers **once**, in a quiet controlled setting. Store the clips in an internal archive. Generate a **travel pack** of approved voice clones for rehearsal and demo use only. Then travel with the voice pack and your tracker patch, rather than trying to clone people on the road from inconsistent phone audio.

That gives you much more stable results, because instant-clone systems can work from short audio, but they still inherit the defects of the source recording. [cite: turn632349search8turn496678search0]

#### My recommendation

For your specific use case, I would set the target at **about 2 minutes per actor for the first pass**, because that sits comfortably inside current instant-clone guidance and is still easy to collect from a company. Then, for any actor whose voice becomes central to the system, I would upgrade that actor later to a **10–30+ minute house dataset**. That is the most efficient path.

If you want, I can turn this into a **theatre-company voice capture sheet** with `$ACTOR_ID`, `$SCRIPT_ID`, `$TARGET_DURATION`, `$USAGE_SCOPE`, and `$VOICE_PROFILE_ID` fields so you can run the recordings cleanly.

### $THEATRE_COMPANY_VOICE_CAPTURE_SHEET

#### $DOCUMENT_PURPOSE

This sheet is designed to create a clean, repeatable company voice library for rehearsal, travel training, internal synthesis tests, and tracker-based dialogue playback.

The five required control fields are:

`$ACTOR_ID`
`$SCRIPT_ID`
`$TARGET_DURATION`
`$USAGE_SCOPE`
`$VOICE_PROFILE_ID`

The sheet below keeps those fixed while adding only the minimum supporting fields needed to run sessions cleanly.

---

#### 1. $CONTROL_VARS

```text
$COMPANY_ID=
$PROJECT_ID=
$SESSION_ID=
$CAPTURE_DATE=
$CAPTURE_LOCATION=
$ENGINE_TARGET=
$AUDIO_FORMAT=
$SAMPLE_RATE=
$BIT_DEPTH=
$MIC_ID=
$ROOM_ID=
$ENGINEERING_OWNER=
$ARCHIVE_ROOT=
$CONSENT_POLICY_VERSION=
```

Example:

```text
$COMPANY_ID=omni-dromenon-machina

$CAPTURE_LOCATION=Hoboken-Studio-A
$ENGINE_TARGET=travel-training-clone-pack
$AUDIO_FORMAT=wav
$SAMPLE_RATE=48000
$BIT_DEPTH=24
$MIC_ID=mkh416
$ROOM_ID=booth-A
$ENGINEERING_OWNER=4444j99
$ARCHIVE_ROOT=/Volumes/$ARCHIVE_DRIVE/company-voice-library
$CONSENT_POLICY_VERSION=v1.0.0
```

---

#### 2. $USAGE_SCOPE_CONTROLLED_VOCABULARY

Use one of these exact values in `$USAGE_SCOPE` so the library remains machine-sortable and policy-clean.

| `$USAGE_SCOPE` | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `internal-training-only` | rehearsal, study, timing practice, travel use |
| `internal-demo-only` | private concept demos inside company or invited circle |
| `internal-production-dev` | internal staging, table reads, cue testing |
| `client-demo-restricted` | external demo allowed only for named project stakeholders |
| `public-release-prohibited` | may not be used in public release contexts |
| `public-release-approved` | performer has approved public synthetic usage |
| `research-only` | internal experiments only; no distribution |

For your stated use case, the default should usually be:

```text
$USAGE_SCOPE=internal-training-only
```

---

#### 3. $VOICE_PROFILE_ID_NAMING_RULE

Use a stable ID shape so voices remain portable across engines.

```text
$VOICE_PROFILE_ID=$ACTOR_ID__$STYLE_CLASS__$TIER__$REVISION
```

Example:

```text
$VOICE_PROFILE_ID=actor-001__neutral-rehearsal__travel-tier__v1
$VOICE_PROFILE_ID=actor-002__measured-stage__house-tier__v2
```

Recommended `$STYLE_CLASS` values:

| `$STYLE_CLASS` | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `neutral-rehearsal` | flat, clean, low-expression capture |
| `measured-stage` | controlled theatrical energy |
| `heightened-stage` | stronger dramatic coloration |
| `narration-clean` | evenly paced explanatory reading |
| `tracker-shortform` | short clusters and fragments for sequencer triggering |

Recommended `$TIER` values:

| `$TIER` | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `travel-tier` | short capture for rehearsal/training |
| `house-tier` | fuller company library |
| `production-tier` | highest-quality approved voice pack |

|---|---|---|---|---:|---:|---|---|---|---|---|

This keeps the script pool stable and reusable.

| `$SCRIPT_ID` | `$SCRIPT_TITLE` | `$SCRIPT_CLASS` | `$TARGET_DURATION` | `$TEXT_OWNER` | `$NOTES` |
|---|---|---|---:|---|---|

| script-002 | Measured Stage Passage | measured-stage | 00:02:00 | company | moderate dramatic energy |

A clean starter pack for each actor is therefore:

`script-001` for neutral base voice
`script-002` for theatrical energy

Example:


---

#### 9. $RECORDING_PROTOCOL

##### 9.1 Before capture

Confirm `$USAGE_SCOPE`, `$CONSENT_STATUS`, `$SCRIPT_ID`, and `$VOICE_PROFILE_ID` before the microphone goes live.

##### 9.2 During capture

Record each script twice if the first take is good, three times if it is unstable. Stop immediately on mouth noise, room interruption, plosive burst, or performance drift.

##### 9.3 After capture

Mark one take `select`, one take `hold`, and all others `reject` or `retake`. Do not leave status ambiguous.

---

#### 10. $RECOMMENDED_DURATION_PLAN

For your travel-training use case, this is the cleanest baseline.

| `$SCRIPT_ID` | `$SCRIPT_CLASS` | `$TARGET_DURATION` |
|---|---|---:|
| script-001 | neutral-rehearsal | 00:02:00 |
| script-002 | measured-stage | 00:02:00 |
| script-003 | tracker-shortform | 00:01:00 |

Total per actor:

```text
$TARGET_DURATION_TOTAL=00:05:00
```

That is enough for a lean internal rehearsal pack while staying easy to collect in one company session.

---

#### 11. $CSV_HEADERS

If you want to paste this directly into Sheets, Airtable, Notion, or a database import, use these headers.

##### $MASTER_CAPTURE_SHEET_HEADERS

```csv
$ACTOR_ID,$ACTOR_NAME,$SCRIPT_ID,$SCRIPT_CLASS,$TARGET_DURATION,$ACTUAL_DURATION,$USAGE_SCOPE,$VOICE_PROFILE_ID,$CONSENT_STATUS,$TAKE_STATUS,$NOTES
```

##### $SCRIPT_REGISTRY_HEADERS

```csv
$SCRIPT_ID,$SCRIPT_TITLE,$SCRIPT_CLASS,$TARGET_DURATION,$TEXT_OWNER,$NOTES
```

##### $TAKE_LOG_HEADERS

```csv
$ACTOR_ID,$SCRIPT_ID,$TAKE_ID,$TARGET_DURATION,$START_TC,$END_TC,$CLIP_FILENAME,$NOISE_STATUS,$DELIVERY_STATUS,$SELECTION_STATUS,$NOTES
```

---

#### 12. $SHORT_OPERATING_VERSION

If you want the absolute minimum sheet, use this:

| `$ACTOR_ID` | `$SCRIPT_ID` | `$TARGET_DURATION` | `$USAGE_SCOPE` | `$VOICE_PROFILE_ID` |
|---|---|---:|---|---|
| actor-001 | script-001 | 00:02:00 | internal-training-only | actor-001__neutral-rehearsal__travel-tier__v1 |
| actor-001 | script-002 | 00:02:00 | internal-training-only | actor-001__measured-stage__travel-tier__v1 |
| actor-001 | script-003 | 00:01:00 | internal-training-only | actor-001__tracker-shortform__travel-tier__v1 |

If you want, I can next turn this into a **Google Sheets-ready TSV** or a **Notion database schema**.

my goal is to have every direction pre-considered for as close to functioning after prompt as capable

## Response

##### Response Segment 1

##### Response Segment 2

Message ID: `0e69cedb-ae4f-4c8b-9e70-d8731dc7ce39`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:57:29.346447Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

#### Yes — but not literally every direction

You will not pre-consider **all** directions. What you can do is make the system **narrow enough, typed enough, and pre-scaffolded enough** that most prompts resolve into already-validated paths instead of improvising new structure each time.

That is the real target: **prompt as selector and filler of prepared structures**, not prompt as inventor of the system from scratch.

#### The design law

For this project, the prompt should sit at the **top** of the stack, not the bottom.

The lower layers should already be fixed: canonical terminology, ontology, object schema, service boundaries, event model, migration shape, testing matrix, and output classes. Your own docs already point in this direction: the system should preserve a stable ontology around `$PHRASE_EVENT`, `$VOICE_CHANNEL`, `$TEMPORAL_RELATION`, `$TIMING_SUBSTRATE`, `$NOTATION_RENDERER`, `$REHEARSAL_KERNEL`, and `$LIVE_EXECUTION_LAYER`, and should not drift into generic voice-tool language. [filecite: turn6file2] [filecite: turn6file4]

So the prompt should not be asked to decide what a scene is, what a version is, what a render is, or what a share artifact is. Those must already be settled. The prompt should only populate or transform them.

#### What “as close to functioning after prompt as possible” actually means

It means the prompt produces **structured, executable deltas** against an already-built system.

For example, after prompt, the system should be able to do things like:

create or modify a scene object,
parse speaker assignments,
save an immutable version,
request a render,
generate diagnostics,
emit audit events,
and expose a shareable review artifact.

Those flows are already the backbone of the planned product: immutable versions, async render jobs, lightweight diagnostics, and read-only share artifacts are all core, not optional polish. [filecite: turn6file0] [filecite: turn6file5] [filecite: turn6file14]

#### The practical implication

You need to move as much uncertainty as possible **out of prompting** and into pre-decided artifacts.

That means having, before any serious prompting phase, at least these things locked:

A canonical lexicon and naming charter, so prompts cannot destabilize ontology. [filecite: turn6file2] [filecite: turn6file16]

A normalized relational core with the split between mutable `scene` state and immutable `scene_version` snapshots, so prompts always write into stable persistence shapes. [filecite: turn6file12] [filecite: turn6file18]

An event model for parse, render, version, diagnostic, and share actions, so every prompt-triggered action becomes observable and debuggable. [filecite: turn6file14]

A fixed service topology — `apps/web`, `apps/api`, `apps/worker`, shared domain/database/config/client packages — so prompts target known boundaries rather than inventing architecture ad hoc. [filecite: turn6file12] [filecite: turn6file17]

A minimum testing matrix for parsing trust, version integrity, render lifecycle correctness, and the end-to-end hearing loop, so “functioning after prompt” means “provable,” not just “plausible.” [filecite: turn6file12]

#### The key shift

The prompt should generate **instances**, not **systems**.

In your system, that means prompting should ideally produce one of four things:

a populated `$SCENE` or `$PHRASE_EVENT_MODEL`,
a transformation of an existing version,
a render request against a known profile,
or a score/rehearsal/export view from already-defined output classes.

Your own concept docs already define those output classes: `$READABLE_SCRIPT`, `$RHYTHMIC_SCORE`, `$SPATIAL_TEXT_SCORE`, `$TABLE_READ_AUDIO`, `$REHEARSAL_PACK`, `$CONDUCTOR_VIEW`, `$LIVE_PROMPT_VIEW`, `$MACHINE_SCORE_OBJECT`, and related render forms. That is exactly the kind of pre-considered output matrix that makes prompting productive instead of speculative. [filecite: turn6file9]

#### The correct prompt posture

So the prompt layer should behave less like:

“Build whatever seems right.”

And more like:

“Given this input, populate these contracts, select these modes, generate these events, and return these outputs.”

That is the only reliable way to get near-immediate function.

#### The product consequence

This also matches the product staircase already in the spec. The first proof is not the total theatrical OS. The first proof is that someone can move from text to hearing quickly, revise, compare versions, and share a reviewable artifact. Then the deeper performance engine grows on top. [filecite: turn6file1] [filecite: turn6file7] [filecite: turn6file10]

So for maximum prompt-readiness, the first prompt-capable surfaces should be narrow and high-value:

scene import,
speaker assignment,
timing defaults,
render request,
diagnostic generation,
version save,
share-link creation.

Everything else can remain latent until the contracts beneath it are solid. [filecite: turn6file6] [filecite: turn6file19]

#### The engineering principle

Your own docs already state the correct sequence: schema and contracts first, then scene import and parse review, then versioning, then render pipeline and playback, then diagnostics and share flows. That sequence is exactly how you make prompt-driven operation trustworthy. If prompting arrives before those layers, it will look impressive and behave inconsistently. [filecite: turn6file12]

#### The shortest formulation

The system should be designed so that:

**the prompt chooses, the contracts constrain, the services execute, the events record, and the tests verify.**

That is how you get closest to “functioning after prompt.”

#### Bottom line

Yes — your goal is right. But the route is not “make prompting smarter.” The route is:

**reduce prompt freedom, increase system preparation, and make every prompt land inside pre-built ontology, contracts, events, and tests.**

That is the architecture most consistent with everything you have already defined. [filecite: turn6file0] [filecite: turn6file2] [filecite: turn6file12]

I can turn this into a concrete **$PROMPT_READYNESS_CHECKLIST** for the repo next.

## Response

Message ID: `a61ee137-7e16-4c6d-8f63-2bf1089d888d`
Created UTC: `2026-03-30T22:17:30.594398Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

#### Direct answer

The right prototype is a **browser-first coded proof**, not a slide deck and not the full theatrical OS. It should prove one tight loop: paste a scene, parse speakers, hear playback, adjust pace and pauses, inspect timing/diagnostics, save a version, and share a read-only review link. That is the smallest proof that still preserves the real product. [filecite: turn3file0] [filecite: turn3file11]

Because tracker-like time is essential for you, the proof should be built as a **hybrid of tracker precision and Ableton-style launch/arrangement control**. In practice, that means the MVP should visibly include a simplified `$PATTERN_VIEW`, a basic `$SESSION_VIEW`, and a basic `$ARRANGEMENT_VIEW`, alongside voice-channels, clip or scene triggering, global tempo, per-event pause values, locator markers, versioned table-read audio, and a first pass at per-cell speech commands. [filecite: turn3file3] [filecite: turn3file16]

#### What the prototype should actually be

At the product surface, keep it framed as a **listening-first revision environment for performative text**. That is the adoptable wedge. Underneath, keep the deeper ontology intact: speech is stored as structured temporal objects, not inert text, so later expansion into overlap, score logic, and live execution remains possible. [filecite: turn3file10] [filecite: turn3file8]

The prototype should therefore center one main workspace. The documented screen model is already correct: a scene import screen, a parse-review screen, then a three-pane scene workspace with script editor on the left, playback and synchronized focus in the center, and speakers, diagnostics, versions, and share actions on the right. That workspace is the product. [filecite: turn3file12] [filecite: turn3file6]

For the tracker part, rows should represent discrete time units, columns should represent `$VOICE_CHANNEL`s, and each cell should hold a `$PHRASE_EVENT` plus command data such as pause, repeat, overlap, cue, density, or transform. The docs already name this direction explicitly: tracker logic should govern micro-form and event control, while Ableton-like views govern launch, arrangement, automation, and macro navigation. [filecite: turn3file16] [filecite: turn3file3]

#### What the first shareable proof of concept should contain

The shareable artifact should not just be “audio export.” It should be a **read-only review page** for a chosen version: playback player, text, speaker labels, and a compact diagnostics summary. The owner can share it externally, but collaborators do not edit there. That makes it legible as a private development instrument rather than a public publishing surface. [filecite: turn3file9] [filecite: turn3file17]

Architecturally, the critical move is the split between mutable `scene` and immutable `scene_version`. The scene is the working draft. The version is the frozen, renderable, analyzable, shareable artifact. Without that split, your proof of concept will feel flimsy; with it, versions, comparisons, and share links become trustworthy. [filecite: turn3file5] [filecite: turn3file2]

#### What to defer

Do **not** try to prove everything at once. The docs are clear that true simultaneous overlap composition, visual score editing, live conductor tooling, deep collaboration, and broader production infrastructure should stay out of MVP. You need the revision loop first, not the full cathedral. [filecite: turn3file1] [filecite: turn3file4]

That means the first proof should show that someone can get from blank scene to first playback quickly, replay multiple times, revise against hearing, save versions, and generate a real share link. That behavioral loop matters more than spectacle. [filecite: turn3file8]

#### Recommended build order

I would build this in three passes.

First, build a **thin but real coded alpha** around one hard-coded or lightly imported sample. Implement `$PATTERN_VIEW`, `$SESSION_VIEW`, and `$ARRANGEMENT_VIEW` as shallow but functional surfaces, with transport, tempo, locator markers, line highlighting, and read-only share output. This proves the tracker/Ableton fusion visually and temporally before you spend energy on broad ingestion. [filecite: turn3file3] [filecite: turn3file16]

Second, add the **core authoring loop**: script paste/import, speaker parsing, speaker assignment, voice assignment, line and scene playback, pace and pause controls, diagnostics, version save, compare, and share. That is the smallest commercially legible product. [filecite: turn3file7] [filecite: turn3file1]

Third, add the first genuinely tracker-native features: per-cell commands, phrase rows, pattern sequencing, and deterministic replay behavior. That is where the product stops feeling like “script playback” and starts feeling like compositional infrastructure. [filecite: turn3file16] [filecite: turn3file3]

#### Technical shape

The underlying system should be browser-first, with a typed API, a queue-backed render worker, relational storage, and object storage for audio. In your own terms, I would treat the minimum infrastructure as `$APP_WEB`, `$APP_API`, `$APP_WORKER`, `$APP_DB`, `$OBJECT_STORE_URI`, and `$TTS_PROVIDER_URI`. What matters most is not the brand choice but preserving structured temporal dialogue objects, version immutability, and fast render turnaround. [filecite: turn3file2] [filecite: turn3file4]

Latency matters more here than in many normal apps. The docs are right: if playback feels like batch processing, the dramaturgical habit breaks. So the proof should prefer short-scene responsiveness, partial re-rendering, and replay of selected regions over heavyweight “export” thinking. [filecite: turn3file1] [filecite: turn3file6]

#### The acceptance test

The proof of concept is successful when a playwright or dramaturg can create a scene, hear it, inspect temporal structure, revise it, save versions, and share a reviewable artifact **without needing other human performers**. The stronger test is whether they choose it over silent rereading for part of their drafting process. [filecite: turn3file8]

#### Bottom line

So: make the prototype a **shareable browser app with three canonical views**. Let `$PATTERN_VIEW` prove tracker time, let `$SESSION_VIEW` prove modular launch and audition, let `$ARRANGEMENT_VIEW` prove long-form structure, and make the read-only share page the public proof. That is the shortest route from your images to a real, legible artifact. [filecite: turn3file16] [filecite: turn3file17]

I can turn this next into a concrete **screen-by-screen prototype spec for `$PATTERN_VIEW`, `$SESSION_VIEW`, `$ARRANGEMENT_VIEW`, and `$SHARE_VIEW`**.

Would this work in max msp or supercollider or something?

## Response

Message ID: `4ff58680-4503-4d86-92e3-e3e36c1506d8`
Created UTC: `2026-03-30T22:45:23.198426Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

#### Yes — but not all in the same role

It would work in **Max/MSP** and it would work in **SuperCollider**, but they are better for different layers of the system.

Your own spec already implies a split between a browser-first product surface and a deeper temporal kernel. The MVP is supposed to be fast, shareable, versioned, and browser-first, while the deeper engine needs a real `$TIMING_SUBSTRATE`, render flow, and later a `$LIVE_EXECUTION_LAYER`. [filecite: turn4file6] [filecite: turn4file3] [filecite: turn4file19]

So the clean answer is:

**use Max/MSP or SuperCollider as engine/prototyping layers, not as the whole product surface.**

#### Which one fits which job

##### Max/MSP

Max is stronger if your immediate goal is a **performable prototype**: transport, cueing, routing, live triggering, conductor logic, text-event playback, spatialization, and interactive control surfaces. That lines up well with the parts of your system concerned with cue logic, click logic, rehearsal support, and live execution. Your concept explicitly needs tempo, subdivision, cue logic, conductor logic, and algorithmic alignment, which is exactly the sort of thing Max is good at in practice. [filecite: turn4file7] [filecite: turn4file19]

If you want to stand in a room and say, “here is the speech-score grid; here are the voices; here is the trigger matrix; here is the timing,” Max is a very plausible first proof.

##### SuperCollider

SuperCollider is stronger if your immediate goal is the **formal timing engine** itself: deterministic scheduling, recurrence, phase offsets, generative transformation, and text-as-score behavior. Your system definition already centers `$PHRASE_EVENT`, `$TEMPORAL_RELATION`, and `$TIMING_SUBSTRATE`; SuperCollider is very well aligned with that deeper, more compositional layer. [filecite: turn4file16] [filecite: turn4file19]

If what you most want to prove is that speech can be sequenced like tracker data — rows, offsets, repeats, canons, cue chains, elastic timing — SuperCollider is the more rigorous engine core.

#### What each one is bad at

Max/MSP is not the ideal place to build the full commercial wedge of the product: projects, versions, diagnostics, parse review, share links, and the browser-first listening-revision workflow. Your MVP docs are explicit that the application surface should be browser-first, with scenes, versions, playback, diagnostics, and share views as first-class product objects. [filecite: turn4file1] [filecite: turn4file3]

SuperCollider is even less suited to being the whole user-facing product. It is powerful as a timing and synthesis/composition engine, but weak as the main environment for the adoptable product layer whose job is “paste a scene, assign speakers, hear it, revise it, save it, share it.” That workflow is the proof point. [filecite: turn4file2] [filecite: turn4file4]

#### The best architecture is hybrid

The strongest shape is:

`$APP_WEB` = browser editor, versions, diagnostics, share links
`$TIMING_ENGINE` = SuperCollider for row-precise scheduling and recurrence
`$LIVE_ENGINE` = Max/MSP for cueing, routing, triggering, rehearsal, and performance control
`$RENDER_WORKER` = async render pipeline for table-read exports
`$BRIDGE_PROTOCOL` = OSC or WebSocket bridge between layers

That architecture matches your system much better than choosing one environment and forcing it to do everything. It also preserves the rule that the system must not collapse into a generic voice wrapper; it keeps language as structured temporal data while still giving you live-performance power. [filecite: turn4file8] [filecite: turn4file12]

#### What I would do first

If the question is **“What gets me to a convincing proof quickly?”**, I would do this:

Build `$PROTO_01` in **Max/MSP** if the main goal is a room-demo: four voice-channels, tracker-like step grid, phrase triggers, tempo map, repeat commands, conductor transport, and simple TTS or prerecorded speech playback. That proves the theatrical-musical mechanism fast.

Build `$PROTO_02` in **SuperCollider** if the main goal is to validate the deeper engine: a row-based `$PHRASE_EVENT` sequencer with deterministic timing, pattern repetition, phase displacement, and render-to-audio output.

Then build `$APP_WEB` only after the engine behavior feels right. The browser app is where the shareable proof becomes legible to playwrights and dramaturgs, but Max or SuperCollider can prove the engine before that.

#### My recommendation

For your exact concern — **time and sequences proceeding like music trackers** — I would lean like this:

**SuperCollider for the tracker-brain.**
**Max/MSP for the live-performance body.**
**Browser app for the commercial/shareable face.**

That is the cleanest division of labor.

If you want the shortest path, start with **Max/MSP** for a visible, audible prototype. If you want the deepest formal correctness, start with **SuperCollider**. If you want the actual product wedge, neither should be the final front end. The product surface still wants to be browser-first. [filecite: turn4file1] [filecite: turn4file17]

#### Bottom line

Yes. It would work.

But the most correct answer is not **“Max or SuperCollider?”**
It is **“Max for live prototyping, SuperCollider for temporal composition logic, browser for the shareable product.”**

I can sketch a concrete `$PROTO_01` architecture in either **Max/MSP-first** or **SuperCollider-first** form.

Created UTC: `2026-03-30T22:48:38.241000Z`

for this part, where i want to show my friend the play from the screenshots above he sent me, as a tracker, triggering thos dialogue clusters through speech synths

## Response

Message ID: `9d687a79-df8c-4140-92de-c0407fcb5b2e`
Created UTC: `2026-03-30T22:49:06.346113Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

Yes. **For that specific demonstration, use Max first.**

Not because Max is metaphysically superior, but because this is a **showable instrument problem**, not yet a product-surface problem. You want to take the play from those screenshots and present it as a tracker-like speech score where dialogue clusters fire through synthesized voices. Max is the fastest route to something your friend can see, hear, and understand in one sitting.

#### What the demo should be

Do **not** build “the app” for this step.

Build a **single-purpose score-player patch**:

A four-lane tracker surface, one lane per voice-channel. The play material is broken into `$PHRASE_EVENT`s and small cluster units. A transport runs top-to-bottom by rows. When the playhead hits an occupied cell, that lane speaks the assigned phrase. That is already fully aligned with the system direction: phrase-events, voice-channels, temporal relation, timing substrate, and score-encoding of speech. [filecite: turn5file17turn5file19]

#### Why Max is the right choice here

For this exact use case, you need three things fast.

First, a visible grid.

Second, deterministic triggering.

Third, immediate audio feedback with controllable routing.

Max gives you those without forcing you to solve the whole browser/version/share problem yet. Your own project material already distinguishes the deeper composition engine from the later product surface, and this is clearly a **composition-and-execution proof**, not yet the full listening-first workbench. [filecite: turn5file1turn5file9]

#### The right prototype shape

Treat the piece as a **tracker score of clustered utterance-events**.

Use these lanes:

`$VOICE_CHANNEL_01 = FIRST_WOMAN`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_02 = SECOND_WOMAN`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_03 = GLASS`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_04 = BAKER`

Then break the text into cluster families rather than preserving only ordinary line order. The screenshots already imply that the work mutates from readable dialogue into distributed repetition fields, so your tracker version should honor that by storing not just full lines, but also decomposed fragments and recombinable clusters. That is exactly the move from literary line to `$PHRASE_EVENT`. [filecite: turn5file7turn5file19]

So your content bank should probably have three levels.

Level one is full-line events for the initial readable exchange.

Level two is phrase clusters for repeated modules.

Level three is atomized fragments for the later dispersal field.

#### What the first demo should look like

The strongest first pass is not a dense Renoise clone. It is a **clean, slow, legible vertical sequencer**.

Across the top: the four voice-channels.

Down the left: row numbers.

In each cell: either empty, a short cluster code, or a compact text label.

At the bottom: tempo, row rate, start/stop, section jump, and a mode selector.

Then give yourself three modes.

Mode one is `$READABLE_PASSAGE`. This plays the scene in conventional order, but as a tracker.

Mode two is `$CLUSTER_PASSAGE`. This begins repeating and redistributing the dialogue clusters.

Mode three is `$DISPERSAL_PASSAGE`. This atomizes the text and spreads fragments across channels and rows.

That alone will make the formal idea obvious to your friend.

#### How to build it in Max

The minimal patch architecture should be:

`[transport]` or a master timing clock
`[metro]` driving row advance
`[counter]` for current row
`[coll]` or `[dict]` storing score rows
`[route]` by voice-channel
speech renderer per channel
mixer and optional pan per channel
a grid UI such as `[matrixctrl]` or a custom Jitter display

The important design decision is that each row should resolve to a small event object, not raw text only. Even in Max, keep the ontology clean.

A row event should look more like this:

```json
{
  "row": 24,
  "voice": "GLASS",
  "text": "need loaf bread",
  "mode": "cluster",
  "pause_after_ms": 180,
  "repeat": 2,
  "pan": 0.2,
  "rate": 1.0
}
```

That keeps the demo structurally faithful to the larger system instead of turning it into a loose trigger toy. Your docs already insist that scenes need to be stored as structured temporal dialogue objects rather than plain documents; this is that principle in miniature. [filecite: turn5file6]

#### Speech synthesis choice

For the demo, you have two viable routes.

Route one is **pre-rendered speech samples**. This is the safest. Render each phrase or cluster once, load them into Max, and trigger them like samples. That gives you stable timing and avoids TTS latency.

Route two is **live speech synthesis**. This is more impressive, but riskier in a demo. If you do this, keep the vocabulary small and cache outputs aggressively.

For showing your friend, I would choose **pre-rendered phrase banks first**. Then the tracker behaves musically and reliably. You are proving the score logic, not the cloud dependency.

#### How to map the screenshots into tracker structure

Do not try to preserve every page visually on day one.

Instead, translate the material into three section blocks.

Section A is the recognizable exchange. One row or one small row-group per utterance. Clear channel identity. Moderate tempo.

Section B is recurrent cluster buildup. Same materials return in alternating or stacked positions. Repeats and offsets become visible.

Section C is distributed field behavior. Phrases fragment into shorter units and appear across channels in a more score-like pattern.

That mapping is already implied by your source concept: text becoming duration, cue, repetition pattern, and voice assignment instead of remaining inert script. [filecite: turn5file15]

#### The specific tracker commands worth using

For this first patch, only implement a few commands.

`PA` for pause after event.
`RP` for repeat count.
`OV` for overlap enable.
`PN` for pan.
`EM` for emphasis or gain boost.
`CU` for cueing another lane one or more rows later.

Those are enough to demonstrate that the piece is not just “read aloud,” but **composed**. Your tracker notes already define this exact shift: row-level commands are where dialogue playback becomes compositional infrastructure. [filecite: turn5file11]

#### What I would not do yet

Do not build version history.

Do not build diagnostics.

Do not build a share link system.

Do not build parse automation from the screenshots.

Just hard-code the play into a tracker-score dataset and make it sing.

That restraint is important. The project materials are clear that the first proof should not get buried under later-layer complexity. [filecite: turn5file2turn5file10]

#### Best concrete plan

Build `$PROTO_PLAY_01` as a Max patch with four speech lanes, about 64 to 128 rows, and three scene states: readable, clustered, dispersed.

Use a single master clock.

Store the score in `dict` or `coll`.

Use pre-rendered phrase audio for reliability.

Display the active row and highlighted cells clearly.

Let your friend mute and solo lanes, change tempo, and jump between the three sections.

That is enough to prove the thesis.

#### Bottom line

For **this part**, yes: use **Max/MSP**.

Make a **trackerized speech-score patch**, not a full app. Treat the dialogue as clustered `$PHRASE_EVENT`s across four `$VOICE_CHANNEL`s, trigger them row-by-row, and let the form evolve from readable exchange into distributed repetition. That will communicate the idea immediately and cleanly. [filecite: turn5file17turn5file19]

I can draft the exact **row schema and section map** for the play next, so you can start patching it directly.


## dramaturgist-tuning - 08 Branch · Prototype & Proof of Concept -- 004 Prompt / Response

File: [dramaturgist-tuning-08--branch-prototype-and-proof-of-concept--004-prompt-response.md](dramaturgist-tuning-08--branch-prototype-and-proof-of-concept--004-prompt-response.md)  
Pair ID: `dt-08-004`  
Content SHA-256: `d079ec4c1cf9671cb674a1832fb8eb2e6247a1daa817fbd67114468cb420221c`

## Prompt

Message ID: `b15365da-03db-4b14-b9a8-8ce59eb8366a`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:44:45.079000Z`

voice synthesis is injectable?

## Response

Message ID: `dbeaf755-a258-4ae0-8b9a-7cbef1a3b448`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:44:52.078691Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

#### Yes

Voice synthesis should be treated as **injectable infrastructure**, not baked into the score model.

The tracker patch should emit a normalized speech event, and then a separate voice layer should decide how that event becomes sound. That keeps the piece portable and lets you swap between pre-rendered samples, OS speech, local synthesis, or remote TTS without changing the score itself.

In your case, the clean separation is:

`$PHRASE_EVENT -> $VOICE_RENDER_ADAPTER -> audio output`

So the tracker only knows things like `$VOICE_CHANNEL`, `$TEXT`, `$ROW`, `$PAUSE_AFTER_MS`, `$RATE`, `$PAN`, `$REPEAT`. It should **not** know whether the sound came from Apple speech, ElevenLabs, a local model, prerecorded WAVs, or anything else.

#### In Max terms

Yes, you can inject voice synthesis into the patch in at least three practical ways.

First, the safest route is `$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE=sample_cache`. The patch triggers pre-rendered phrase files from `$VOICE_CACHE_DIR`. This is best for the demo because timing is stable and there is no network delay.

Second, you can use `$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE=live_tts`. The patch sends text to a synthesis adapter through Node for Max, `js`, OSC, or HTTP, and receives audio back. This is more flexible but less stable in a live demo.

Third, you can use `$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE=hybrid`. If a phrase exists in cache, play it immediately. If not, request synthesis, write the result into `$VOICE_CACHE_DIR`, and use the cached version on the next pass. That is usually the smartest architecture.

#### What “injectable” should mean structurally

It should mean that the same score can run against multiple backends by changing environment variables, not by rewriting the patch.

For example:

`$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE=sample_cache`
`$TTS_PROVIDER_URI=http://127.0.0.1:8787/render`
`$VOICE_CACHE_DIR=/Users/you/audio-cache/speech-score`
`$DEFAULT_VOICE_PROFILE_ID=glass-neutral-01`
`$FALLBACK_VOICE_PROFILE_ID=narrator-flat-01`

Then each lane or `$VOICE_CHANNEL` can also carry its own voice assignment:

`$VOICE_CHANNEL_01_PROFILE=first-woman-bright-01`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_02_PROFILE=second-woman-dry-01`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_03_PROFILE=glass-measured-01`
`$VOICE_CHANNEL_04_PROFILE=baker-warm-01`

That is the right meaning of injectable here: the patch stays the same; the rendering backend changes.

#### For your friend-demo

Use injection, but do it conservatively.

For the first proof, I would not rely on real-time cloud speech. I would inject the voice layer as a **cached renderer**. The tracker fires cluster events; the adapter resolves them to pre-rendered files. That will feel instantaneous and musical.

Then, once the demo works, you can swap `$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE` to live synthesis and test dynamic text generation later.

#### The best internal contract

Make every tracker cell resolve to something like this:

```json
{
  "phrase_id": "glass_024",
  "voice_channel": "GLASS",
  "text": "Yes. I need a loaf of bread.",
  "voice_profile_id": "glass-measured-01",
  "start_row": 24,
  "pause_after_ms": 160,
  "repeat": 1,
  "pan": 0.15,
  "render_mode": "cache_preferred"
}
```

Then the adapter does the rest.

That is the key move. The score remains a score. Voice synthesis becomes a pluggable render service.

#### Bottom line

Yes. It should be injectable.

For your Max prototype, that means: **do not hardwire the synthesis engine into the tracker logic**. Hardwire only the event schema and the timing. Inject the voice backend through `$VOICE_PROVIDER_MODE`, `$TTS_PROVIDER_URI`, `$VOICE_CACHE_DIR`, and per-channel `$VOICE_PROFILE_ID`s.

For the demo you described, cached phrase rendering is the most reliable version of that idea.

how much audio of voice speaking is required to create a synthetic copy of recording?

## Response

##### Response Segment 1

Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:46:03.692763Z`

##### Response Segment 2

Message ID: `54dd9095-f60a-4f0d-b43a-b2f516e17cc5`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:46:23.520571Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

I have enough to answer now. The current providers are all over the map: “instant similarity” clones can work from seconds, while stable long-form or premium clones usually want tens of minutes to hours.

##### Response Segment 3

Message ID: `501fc6e9-f9b7-4126-989c-16db30421e82`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:46:23.520571Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

#### It depends on what you mean by “synthetic copy”

If you mean a **quick, recognizable voice clone**, current systems can often get something usable from **just a few seconds to a couple of minutes** of clean speech. Cartesia’s cloning docs say a high-similarity clone can use a clip of about **5 seconds**, and Resemble’s “rapid clone” is documented at **10 seconds to 3 minutes**. ElevenLabs’ instant voice cloning guidance is stricter and recommends about **1–2 minutes** of clear audio. [cite: turn879704search4turn879704search0turn194595search6turn194595search1

If you mean a **high-quality, stable, production-grade clone** that holds up across longer passages and captures more nuance, the requirement rises a lot. Resemble documents **10–25+ minutes** for professional cloning; Cartesia says its pro clone requires at least **30 minutes** and recommends about **2 hours**; ElevenLabs recommends **30–180 minutes**, with guidance pointing toward **1–3 hours** for best results. citeturn879704search0turn879704search6turn194595search0turn194595search1

#### Practical rule of thumb

For planning purposes, treat it like this: **5–30 seconds** can be enough for a rough similarity demo; **1–3 minutes** is a common target for a decent instant clone; **10–30+ minutes** is where more serious trained clones start; **30–180 minutes** is the range to expect for a near-professional or long-form voice model. citeturn879704search4turn879704search0turn194595search6turn194595search0turn879704search6]

#### What matters as much as duration

Providers repeatedly emphasize that **audio quality and consistency matter at least as much as raw runtime**. They want **one speaker only**, minimal background noise, little reverb, trimmed silence, and speech in the target style and language; otherwise the clone tends to reproduce the recording defects too. ElevenLabs says the total runtime matters more than the number of clips and recommends clear single-speaker audio; Cartesia similarly says longer clips do not necessarily improve the clone and recommends short, clean speech with limited pauses. [cite: turn194595search0turn194595search7turn879704search1

#### For your use case

For a **tracker-style play demo with dialogue clusters**, you do **not** need a perfect long-form clone. A short, recognizable clone or even a stylized synthetic proxy is enough, so **10 seconds to 2 minutes per voice** is usually the realistic minimum zone to test the idea. If you want it to sound convincingly like a specific person across a whole performance, budget **30 minutes or more**, preferably much more. citeturn879704search0turn194595search6turn194595search0]

#### One constraint worth stating plainly

If the voice is **not your own**, many platforms require permission, and some professional-clone workflows restrict creation to the speaker verifying their own voice. ElevenLabs, for example, says instant cloning requires that you have the right and consent to clone the voice, and its professional cloning flow is limited to **your own verified voice**. [cite: turn194595search6turn194595search4turn194595search3]

If you want, I can map this into a concrete capture plan like `4 voices × target sample length × recording settings × cache strategy` for your Max patch.

##### Response Segment 2

Message ID: `f32ba670-bf5f-40f5-b549-2e9ac60ef8a5`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T02:49:01.780975Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

Yes. For **internal rehearsal or training while traveling**, that is a sensible use case — but structure it as a **company voice library with explicit performer permission**, not as ad hoc scraping of old recordings.

#### The practical constraint

The main operational issue is that **provider rules differ**. ElevenLabs’ instant cloning flow requires you to confirm you have the **right and consent** to clone the voice, while ElevenLabs’ **professional** cloning is limited to **your own verified voice** and recommends much longer training audio. That means a theatre company can use instant-style internal clones more easily than “professional” clones on that platform, but not every provider treats third-party company voices the same way. [cite: turn632349search0turn632349search5turn632349search4]

#### The capture target I would use

For your use case, I would split the voice bank into two quality tiers.

For a **travel/training tier**, capture about **1–3 minutes of clean speech per performer**. That is enough to land in the workable range for current “instant” workflows: ElevenLabs recommends roughly **1–2 minutes** of clear audio for instant cloning, Resemble documents **10 seconds** for rapid clone, and Cartesia’s clone endpoint says a high-similarity clone can use about **5 seconds**, with its broader best-practices page recommending clips around **10 seconds**. [cite: turn632349search0turn496678search3turn632349search6turn632349search8]

For a **durable house-library tier**, I would capture **10–30 minutes per performer at minimum**, and preferably more if you want stronger long-form stability. That recommendation is an inference from current provider guidance: Resemble’s professional clone flow calls for **20+ recordings totaling at least 10 minutes**, while ElevenLabs’ professional guidance recommends **30 minutes bare minimum**, with **1–3 hours** preferred for best results. [cite: turn496678search3turn496678search0turn632349search4]

#### The recording spec

What matters almost as much as runtime is **cleanliness and consistency**. The docs are very consistent on this point: use **one speaker only**, keep the room quiet, avoid reverb, avoid long silences, and keep the delivery style reasonably consistent because the model will imitate the speaking style, pacing, and artifacts it hears. ElevenLabs and Cartesia both explicitly say noise, pauses, and performance style will be reflected in the clone. [cite: turn632349search0turn496678search0turn632349search8]

So for each actor, I would record one neutral rehearsal script, one expressive script closer to their stage energy, and one short “control” script with the exact diction you care about for the piece. Then I would keep only the cleanest takes and trim them tightly.

#### The best company workflow

For your theatre-company case, the cleanest model is this:

Record the performers **once**, in a quiet controlled setting. Store the clips in an internal archive. Generate a **travel pack** of approved voice clones for rehearsal and demo use only. Then travel with the voice pack and your tracker patch, rather than trying to clone people on the road from inconsistent phone audio.

That gives you much more stable results, because instant-clone systems can work from short audio, but they still inherit the defects of the source recording. [cite: turn632349search8turn496678search0]

#### My recommendation

For your specific use case, I would set the target at **about 2 minutes per actor for the first pass**, because that sits comfortably inside current instant-clone guidance and is still easy to collect from a company. Then, for any actor whose voice becomes central to the system, I would upgrade that actor later to a **10–30+ minute house dataset**. That is the most efficient path.

If you want, I can turn this into a **theatre-company voice capture sheet** with `$ACTOR_ID`, `$SCRIPT_ID`, `$TARGET_DURATION`, `$USAGE_SCOPE`, and `$VOICE_PROFILE_ID` fields so you can run the recordings cleanly.

### $THEATRE_COMPANY_VOICE_CAPTURE_SHEET

#### $DOCUMENT_PURPOSE

This sheet is designed to create a clean, repeatable company voice library for rehearsal, travel training, internal synthesis tests, and tracker-based dialogue playback.

The five required control fields are:

`$ACTOR_ID`
`$SCRIPT_ID`
`$TARGET_DURATION`
`$USAGE_SCOPE`
`$VOICE_PROFILE_ID`

The sheet below keeps those fixed while adding only the minimum supporting fields needed to run sessions cleanly.

---

#### 1. $CONTROL_VARS

```text
$COMPANY_ID=
$PROJECT_ID=
$SESSION_ID=
$CAPTURE_DATE=
$CAPTURE_LOCATION=

```

#### 2. $USAGE_SCOPE_CONTROLLED_VOCABULARY

Use one of these exact values in `$USAGE_SCOPE` so the library remains machine-sortable and policy-clean.

| `$USAGE_SCOPE` | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `internal-training-only` | rehearsal, study, timing practice, travel use |
| `internal-demo-only` | private concept demos inside company or invited circle |
| `internal-production-dev` | internal staging, table reads, cue testing |
| `client-demo-restricted` | external demo allowed only for named project stakeholders |
| `public-release-prohibited` | may not be used in public release contexts |
| `public-release-approved` | performer has approved public synthetic usage |
| `research-only` | internal experiments only; no distribution |

For your stated use case, the default should usually be:

```text
$USAGE_SCOPE=internal-training-only
```

#### 3. $VOICE_PROFILE_ID_NAMING_RULE

Use a stable ID shape so voices remain portable across engines.

```text
$VOICE_PROFILE_ID=$ACTOR_ID__$STYLE_CLASS__$TIER__$REVISION
```

Example:

```text
$VOICE_PROFILE_ID=actor-001__neutral-rehearsal__travel-tier__v1
$VOICE_PROFILE_ID=actor-002__measured-stage__house-tier__v2
```

Recommended `$STYLE_CLASS` values:

| `$STYLE_CLASS` | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `neutral-rehearsal` | flat, clean, low-expression capture |
| `measured-stage` | controlled theatrical energy |
| `heightened-stage` | stronger dramatic coloration |
| `narration-clean` | evenly paced explanatory reading |
| `tracker-shortform` | short clusters and fragments for sequencer triggering |

Recommended `$TIER` values:

| `$TIER` | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `travel-tier` | short capture for rehearsal/training |
| `house-tier` | fuller company library |
| `production-tier` | highest-quality approved voice pack |

This keeps the script pool stable and reusable.

```text
$MASTER_CLIP_FILENAME=$VOICE_PROFILE_ID__master.$AUDIO_FORMAT
```

Example:

```text
actor-001__tracker-shortform__travel-tier__v1__master.wav
```

---

#### 9. $RECORDING_PROTOCOL

##### 9.1 Before capture

Confirm `$USAGE_SCOPE`, `$CONSENT_STATUS`, `$SCRIPT_ID`, and `$VOICE_PROFILE_ID` before the microphone goes live.

##### 9.2 During capture

Record each script twice if the first take is good, three times if it is unstable. Stop immediately on mouth noise, room interruption, plosive burst, or performance drift.

##### 9.3 After capture

Mark one take `select`, one take `hold`, and all others `reject` or `retake`. Do not leave status ambiguous.

---

#### 10. $RECOMMENDED_DURATION_PLAN

For your travel-training use case, this is the cleanest baseline.

| `$SCRIPT_ID` | `$SCRIPT_CLASS` | `$TARGET_DURATION` |
|---|---|---:|
| script-001 | neutral-rehearsal | 00:02:00 |
| script-002 | measured-stage | 00:02:00 |
| script-003 | tracker-shortform | 00:01:00 |

Total per actor:

```text
$TARGET_DURATION_TOTAL=00:05:00
```

That is enough for a lean internal rehearsal pack while staying easy to collect in one company session.

---

#### 11. $CSV_HEADERS

If you want to paste this directly into Sheets, Airtable, Notion, or a database import, use these headers.

##### $MASTER_CAPTURE_SHEET_HEADERS

```csv
$ACTOR_ID,$ACTOR_NAME,$SCRIPT_ID,$SCRIPT_CLASS,$TARGET_DURATION,$ACTUAL_DURATION,$USAGE_SCOPE,$VOICE_PROFILE_ID,$CONSENT_STATUS,$TAKE_STATUS,$NOTES
```

##### $SCRIPT_REGISTRY_HEADERS

```csv
$SCRIPT_ID,$SCRIPT_TITLE,$SCRIPT_CLASS,$TARGET_DURATION,$TEXT_OWNER,$NOTES
```

##### $TAKE_LOG_HEADERS

```csv
$ACTOR_ID,$SCRIPT_ID,$TAKE_ID,$TARGET_DURATION,$START_TC,$END_TC,$CLIP_FILENAME,$NOISE_STATUS,$DELIVERY_STATUS,$SELECTION_STATUS,$NOTES
```

---

#### 12. $SHORT_OPERATING_VERSION

If you want the absolute minimum sheet, use this:

| `$ACTOR_ID` | `$SCRIPT_ID` | `$TARGET_DURATION` | `$USAGE_SCOPE` | `$VOICE_PROFILE_ID` |
|---|---|---:|---|---|
| actor-001 | script-001 | 00:02:00 | internal-training-only | actor-001__neutral-rehearsal__travel-tier__v1 |
| actor-001 | script-002 | 00:02:00 | internal-training-only | actor-001__measured-stage__travel-tier__v1 |
| actor-001 | script-003 | 00:01:00 | internal-training-only | actor-001__tracker-shortform__travel-tier__v1 |

If you want, I can next turn this into a **Google Sheets-ready TSV** or a **Notion database schema**.


## dramaturgist-tuning - 08 Branch · Prototype & Proof of Concept -- 008 Prompt / Response

File: [dramaturgist-tuning-08--branch-prototype-and-proof-of-concept--008-prompt-response.md](dramaturgist-tuning-08--branch-prototype-and-proof-of-concept--008-prompt-response.md)  
Pair ID: `dt-08-008`  
Content SHA-256: `8d340eb6d926f846dfa786fe5a9c9b453cb2a46164cb7a6c4a3574d86ae4c425`

## Prompt

Message ID: `90988b45-2ab9-43e2-908b-4814504f7258`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T03:00:35.831000Z`

reduce prompt freedom, increase system preparation, and make every prompt land inside pre-built ontology, contracts, events, and tests.

## Response

##### Response Segment 1

Message ID: `0b9e19ab-ac10-46fd-8cdb-06864c36280d`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T03:00:37.200307Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

I’m pulling the exact architectural hooks for this so the answer lands as a concrete operating rule, not just a slogan.

##### Response Segment 2

Message ID: `0d43cd66-1de1-406c-bb09-b9a6d35732d8`
Created UTC: `2026-03-31T03:00:57.195983Z`
Model: `gpt-5-4-thinking`

#### Yes

That is the correct direction.

The system should become **less prompt-driven and more model-driven**. Prompts should not be allowed to define the ontology on the fly. They should only select from, populate, and transform **predeclared system objects** inside the canonical architecture. That is already consistent with your terminology charter, which makes the ontology prior to UI convenience and requires canonical internal terms to remain stable across schema, contracts, architecture, and code. [filecite: turn1file0] [filecite: turn1file4] [filecite: turn1file3]

#### The governing rule

Use this as the rule:

```text id="6c1gsu"
$PROMPT_FREEDOM=low
$SYSTEM_PREPARATION=high
$PROMPT_ROLE=selector-and-instantiator
$ONTOLOGY_SOURCE=prebuilt
$CONTRACT_SOURCE=prebuilt
$EVENT_SOURCE=prebuilt
$TEST_SOURCE=prebuilt
```

In plain terms: the prompt should not invent what the system is. The system already knows what a `$PHRASE_EVENT`, `$VOICE_CHANNEL`, `$TIMING_SUBSTRATE`, `$ANALYSIS_ENGINE`, and `$REHEARSAL_KERNEL` are. The prompt only chooses which of those objects to create, update, compare, render, or analyze. [filecite: turn1file4]

#### The architectural consequence

The prompt should enter the system **after** ontology, contracts, and events already exist.

So the flow becomes:

```text id="0j2z7x"
user_intent
-> canonical_term_resolution
-> contract_validation
-> domain_action
-> event_emission
-> invariant_checks
-> test_execution
-> state_commit
-> render_or_share
```

That is exactly the shape implied by the repo package layout: canonical schemas and value objects in `packages/domain/`, explicit `events/` and `contracts/`, SQL-backed persistence, typed API boundaries, and a CI path that checks shared contract drift, parsing trust, version integrity, and render lifecycle correctness. [filecite: turn1file6] [filecite: turn1file10]

#### The design law

A good formulation would be:

> **Prompts are not miniature product specifications. Prompts are bounded requests against a prepared speech-score system.**

Or more formally:

> **Every prompt must resolve into an allowed operation over canonical objects, validated by typed contracts, recorded as domain events, and checked against invariants and tests before trusted state changes occur.**

That is the strongest version of what you are asking for. It also aligns with the style guide’s requirement that architecture documents be declarative, explicit about boundaries and invariants, and system-first rather than hand-wavy or improvisational. [filecite: turn1file3]

#### What this means operationally

A prompt should never be allowed to say, in effect, “make up whatever structure seems useful.”

Instead, it should be forced to land in one of a finite set of prepared domains such as:

`$SCENE_PARSE_REQUEST`
`$VERSION_CREATE_REQUEST`
`$RENDER_REQUEST`
`$DIAGNOSTIC_REQUEST`
`$SHARE_CREATE_REQUEST`
`$PATTERN_EDIT_REQUEST`
`$SESSION_LAUNCH_REQUEST`
`$ARRANGEMENT_EDIT_REQUEST`

That same discipline already appears in your event model. The system does not just “do render stuff”; it emits `render.requested`, `render.queued`, `render.processing`, `render.completed`, and `render.failed`. It does not just “save work”; it emits `version.created`, `version.restored`, and `version.compared`. That is the correct pattern: prompts map into prepared eventful operations, not fuzzy magic. [filecite: turn1file1]

#### The trust consequence

This matters because trust in your system depends on **deterministic state and bounded meaning**, not on clever prompting.

Your own implementation notes already identify the core trust zones as parsing trust, version integrity, and render lifecycle correctness. The repo is supposed to encode these in tests across parsing, versioning, rendering, and the end-to-end scene-hearing loop. That means prompts must be subordinated to those trust zones, not allowed to bypass them. [filecite: turn1file10]

The most important example is the split between mutable `scene` and immutable `scene_version`. A prompt should not directly mutate trusted history. It should mutate working state, then pass through version creation rules if the result is to become a stable artifact. That immutability boundary is one of the system’s strongest architectural moves. [filecite: turn1file8]

#### The product consequence

This also protects the product from collapsing into commodity voice tooling.

If prompts remain too free, the system starts behaving like an undifferentiated LLM wrapper with speech output. If prompts are instead forced through a prepared ontology of temporal dramatic language, the moat stays where it belongs: in the structured data model, the revision loop, the diagnostics layer, and the score-execution architecture. [filecite: turn1file16] [filecite: turn1file14]

#### The sentence to keep

Use this internally:

> **Reduce prompt freedom. Increase system preparation. Require every prompt to terminate in canonical ontology, typed contracts, explicit domain events, and passing tests.**

Or even tighter:

```text id="f8qlsr"
$PROMPTS_SHALL_NOT_DEFINE_THE_SYSTEM
$PROMPTS_SHALL_SELECT_WITHIN_THE_SYSTEM
```

#### The clean implementation doctrine

If you want the doctrine in strict repo language, it should read like this:

```text id="t8wt42"
All prompt-handling surfaces shall resolve user input into canonical domain objects and approved operations only. No prompt may introduce ad hoc ontology, bypass typed contracts, skip event emission, or mutate trusted state without passing invariant checks and the relevant test path. Prompt interpretation is a front-door convenience layer; ontology, contracts, events, and tests remain the real system.
```

That is the right move. It makes the prompt thin and the system thick.

[filecite: turn1file0] [filecite: turn1file6] [filecite: turn1file10]

If you want, I can turn that next into a formal **`$PROMPT_GOVERNANCE_CHARTER`** artifact in the same repo style.


## dramaturgist-tuning - 08 Branch · Prototype & Proof of Concept -- 009 Prompt / Response
